Oh you, very dear sons, I have completed this book which is the end of the knowledge of medicine along with its contents, and I have finished it through its explanations and by its declarations. And it came to me in vision that I would complete it for you with this treatise, which is part of the work of hands, namely "surgery".
And because the work of hands is forgotten in our region and is completely unknown everywhere, so the science has perished and been lost, and nothing remains from it except the small descriptions in books by the ancients that hands have moved. And because of this has come error and vanity, and intentions are closed off and true knowledge is estranged.
Now it seems to me that I will revive it along with the ordering of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and I will come along with the forms of surgical instruments of cautery and other tools of work with the adjustment of the declaration and preparation for them, as is necessary.
And because there are no good masters of hands in our time, the art of medicine has been estranged. And it is necessary for someone who undertakes this to ascend to the science of anatomy, as Galien says, so long as he knows the justice of members, and their forms and conjunctions and separations, and has knowledge of bones and nerves and sinews and the number of them, and their arrangement and moving and resting veins, and from where their origin is.
And because of this Hippocrates says that the name with its name is much, and with its work is little, and similarly in the art of hand.
We warn you in the introduction of this book that whoever is not knowledgeable about the things mentioned above should guard himself lest he fall into error and harm people; thus I have often warned many who undertake this science and abandon their knowledge and experience.
For I saw a foolish physician who cut, that is to say, an abscess of scrofula or the nape of a woman's neck, and cut the arteries of the neck; why then so much blood came out that the woman fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician who treated an ulcer of an elderly man's leg, and the ulcer was large; so he took with him a piece of the bladder, and the man died within three days.
And I had already been called to treat him, and I saw the state of the sick person and the size of the ulcer, and I knew that if they were to cut it out, he would die.
And I saw another physician who undertook to treat the leg of a duke from our land, which was broken and had an open wound; and the ignorant doctor applied splints to the break over the wound by force, and did not leave any space for antiseptic treatment, and thus left it in that state until the leg became completely infected and gangrenous, and came to ruin.
I was called to come to him, and I hurried to come and relieve the leg; and when I removed the splints, he was at peace and did not feel any pain.
But in every way the corruption was already confirmed in the member, and I could not heal it; and the corruption did not cease to spread through the member until the entire member was lost.
And I saw another physician who drained a suppurating abscess, and then inflamed it further through the wickedness of his treatment.
For an ulcer, when it is pure of melancholic humor, it does not seem fitting that one touch it with iron unless it is in such a place where one can entirely remove what is corrupted.
And therefore, dear son, it is necessary for you to know that the work of one's hands is divided into two divisions: namely, a work where there is danger in multiple dispositions; and I will show in each place of this book where there is fear and danger.
Why do you need to keep them safe so that the foolish gazer cannot speak evil of you.
Take your arms in custody, and begin your treatments lightly and firmly, and use the best method so that through the grace of God you may come to salvation and be praised; and leave frightful diseases with miserable health; and take away from you what makes you doubt, and have courage within yourself so that you can come out of this world into the other one full of glory.
Galen said in some of his admonitions: "Do not take on the treatment of a bad disease unless you are called a foolish doctor."
I have divided this treatise into three chapters.
The first chapter is about cauterizing with fire and medicine poultices, and it is the chapter ordered from head to foot; and the forms of the instruments, and the handles of the cauterizer, and all that is necessary in the work.
The second chapter is about cutting and piercing, and the application of leeches and wounds, and the removal of splinters and similar things; and it is the chapter ordered with the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter is about restoring dislocations and sprains, and similar things; and it is ordered from head to foot, with the forms of the instruments.
So that we remember the work, it is necessary for me to tell the quality of the help and the harm of each temperament.
I say that the quality of the help and the harm is the longest word and most subtle science and secret response; and many wise people have spoken about this, and they agreed with each other; and I was not given much time to speak, because I fear the length.
I say that cauterizing helps universally in all constitutions where there is material, except for two constitutions: namely, the hot constitution with material, and the dry constitution with material.
In the hot and dry constitution with material, some people said that cauterizing is great help, while others said the opposite: that cauterizing is not worth it for illnesses that come from heat and dryness.
For the nature of fire is hot, and it should not be cured with heat or dryness itself; and those who said the opposite said that cauterizing makes great help for hot and dry illnesses that come into men's bodies. For when you compare the body of man to its moisture in the constitution of fire, you find the body cold.
And I agree with this statement. For I have found it to be true at times through experience.
And in any case, one should not meddle with it except for those who have tested it and know through long experience the chapter of cauterizing, and the diversity of human constitutions, and their dispositions and causes of illnesses, and cures and the pace of time; as for other complexions, we are not afraid of them, especially in cold and moist diseases. And all the doctors gathered together and agreed on cauterizing.
And know, son, that cauterizing with fire is a secret of the cure, and of greater goodness than cauterizing with burning medicine.
For fire is simple, and it does not harm except the member that is burned, and it does not hurt another member if you know its name.
The burning medicines spread from one member to another and bring grievous and difficult-to-cure diseases to the afflicted member, and perhaps kill the sick person.
Fire, because of its purity and goodness of its substance, does not do these things, unless there is an excess.
And I have found this to be true through experience and the length of service and study that I have put into the art, and it is in agreement with the truth of things. And for these reasons, I excuse myself from the length of my words, because I do not want to take away the secret which is answered by fire, and the quality of the work, how it cures diseases of the body, as the philosopher says in demonstrating it; accept this in your thoughts.
And know, son, that they agreed on what time the cauterizations should be done, and appointed assistants.
And I say that cauterization is suitable at any time, because the damage caused by them happens below and is removed by the aid of the cauterization; and indeed if the cauterizations are for necessary and urgent pains that cannot wait for the time when greater evil will come to the member.
And never think about it, dear son, as foolish people do; for the disease that is healed by cauterization does not have a relapse; and they say so themselves; but it is not so as they believe, because cauterizations are not just medicine that moves the complexion, and dries up the humors that cause the pain, and counts more than medicine in its speed of value, and in the virtue of its work and its power.
And it is possible that the disease comes sometimes according to the complexion of the sick person and the place of the disease and its virtue; and excesses are gathered in him through the acquisition of foods and similar things, unless cauterizations are administered at a place where there is little excess and moisture, such as cauterizations of dens for pain, and the like things; and that kind of pain does not come back again; but it happens sometimes.
And many people do not understand why cauterizations are called the last resort of medicine, and that is true; but they do not believe that cauterization is worth after medicine, but their intentions are false, and cauterization is considered the last resort of medicine because when we administer ways of cures in any disease and they do not work, then we administer the cauterization, and it works. Therefore it is clearly seen that cauterization is the last resort of medicine, not according to the intention of foolish doctors.
The ancients said that cauterizations made with a gold instrument are more effective than those made with iron, and they did not say this only because of the quality of gold and its high substance; and they said that the place of cauterization does not cause corruption, but that is not true because I have tried it and found that in some people it is true; and a gold cauterization is more effective than an iron one, as they said.
And besides, when you heat an iron instrument made of gold, you cannot tell if it is hot enough just by looking at the color of the gold, and it cools down immediately; and if it is heated too much, it becomes brittle; and thus there are many hindrances.
And therefore we use iron cauterizations because they have been made more convenient to our work.
Now I have ordered this chapter from the head to the foot so that the view is easier to find.
The oil cautery.
These cauteries are used for moist and cold on the brain, and there are two which are done because of an illness called "soda", and due to many humors that descend from the head towards themselves, and towards the ears, and for a multitude of dreams and pain in the teeth and throat, and similarly to all illnesses that come from coldness such as paralysis and dropsy and apoplexy and similar illnesses.
The form of this cautery is that the patient be purged by a dissolving medicine, cleansing the head three or four times, depending on what virtue and age and custom of the patient can endure; and then reapply the head of the patient.
And then make him sit cross-legged between your hands, and put his hands on the scales; after that place the root of your palm over the root of his navel between his two eyes; and let blood come from the middle vein, then heat the cautery called "olivare", which is in this form:
And place it on the marked spot with ink, and move your hand slowly and evenly in a circular motion; then lift your hand suddenly, and reward the spot if you see the bone 1 small span's length; and if you don't see it, redo the cautery until you see the bone as I told you; after that take 1 spoonful of silk, moisten and soften cotton with it, and place it on the spot, and let it stay for 3 days; then put a wet linen compress in a burrow; and leave it on until the heat is gone; after that treat the wound with the ointment called "tetrafarmacium" until it's healed.
And the old man said that the longer and more open the wound is, the more it purges itself; and for this reason it is beneficial.
And one of them said that the cautery should be left in place until part of the bone is charred, and then remove what has been burned off from the bone, and treat it.
And the other one said that the cautery should be left in place until it is pressed so hard against the bone that an area as wide as a spindle (used by women to spin thread) is charred, so that the humors can flow out; and the wound should be left open for a long time; after that it should be treated and cooked.
And I think that both of these methods of cauterization are not good in all cases, except perhaps in people with a clear sign of fear; and it seems to me that I should leave them alone, because the flesh is weakened by continued natural solutions, as we have seen in other members; and similarly, when the patient's flesh is naturally weak and the first appearance of cauterization is more serious and painful for me, and with this I work towards a similar recovery.

the headache

Of the pain of the ears due to coldness.

Of the torture of the mouth.
Torture that must be cured by cauterization is none other than those that move from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of disease; and do not make a cauterization for the semblances that come from heat and spasm of nerves.
When you cure this semblance of torture that moves from phlegm and purgations of the head and gargarism, and it does not help, then it is necessary that you cauterize the ailing area with 3 cauters: one at the root of the ear, and another a little behind the temple, and the third in the joining of the two lips; and put the cauter on the part that appears to be healthy, and make the cauter hot near the corner of the head, above a little below the horn of the head, and another in the temple as long as one finger; and leave the cauter so high that the middle of the wound is burned. De litargie.
Give the patient large doses of herbs and purging pills to clear the head; after, rinse the patient's head thoroughly, and apply a mustard plaster on the back of the head as described in the treatise on plasters; and this is the method for cauterizing: do it just as I have said.
If it is well done like this, then the patient will be good; and if not, make three cauterizations on the head below the neck, with a space of two large pieces between each one, and treat it just as the others, and if you see that it is inflamed, and the patient is suffering, make one cauterization in the middle; and if necessary, two more above the two horns, and treat it until it is healed; and this cauterization which is called "olivare" as I said before.

the eyelash which points the eye At times it happens that the eyelashes are converted to stinging, and they prick the eye, and one makes a cautery in two manners: namely with fire, or with burning medicine.
Or the cautery of fire commands the patient to leave the lashes alone until they are long and even; and if they prick at their birth, then he binds them so that they do not move until they are born.
When they are born and are even, then the patient puts honey on them; afterward he writes a figure on the parchment of the eyes that is as the leaf of myrtle, and it is the beginning of the sign near the lashes; then he puts moistened cotton in egg-glue and muskalliance of persimmon on the eye; then he heats the cautery of which this is the form:
Afterward cauterize on the figure that you have signed with a small dot and dot many times, so that the cautery is burned in that place; and the sign of the health of the eye is that you see the parchment retracted, and the lashes raised to the point of the eye; then lift your hand, and leave it thus for three days, then put moistened cotton in butter until the scabbing is removed; afterward heal with ointment until it is healed.
And if after this the eyelashes come and the parchment is softened, then start again with the cautery on that same spot, just as you did first; and if the lashes are too long, then cauterize until the parchment rises to its natural level, and is even, and no longer pricks the eye.
The burning cautery of medicine is that you instruct the patient to let the hairs grow long and even; then make a parchment shape like the leaf of myrtle; then take French soap and living wax, each one-third, and grind them together well; and do it quickly so it doesn't cool down, then spread it over the shape that you have made as the leaf of myrtle, and place it on paper under the eyes; and if the disease is in only one eye, then work in one, and also in both; and place moist cotton under the eye like an egg white; and the patient's face be in your hand and place your thumb showing above the medicine, and press one spot and the whole egg all the times that the patient feels the burning of the medicine, for he feels burning as well as fire. When you have done this for a long time, then remove the medicine from your thumb. And when the burning has passed, then lift up the cloth of cotton wool, and reward yourself if you see the paper lifted as well as if it were lifted by constraint of fire or incision; and if so, then it is good. If not, then put more medicine on the spot where the medicine did not work, until your works are equal, and your eyes are in order; then place moist cotton on the burn until the burns have cooled, afterwards cure the spot with ointment called "dyafenis" and others, until it is healed.

Of the treatment of a fistula by cauterization
When you treat a fistula as we have said in the division of diseases, and your treatments do not help, then it is necessary that you make it cauterize in this manner.
Command the patient to put his head in your hand; and have the servant hold the cauterizer in such a way that it does not move, nor leave his head; then put moist cotton on one eye with egg white or persillum juice; then heat the cauterizer which is so shaped: and shape it like the tip of a quill pen; whether you wish to cauterize from one end to the other, or as an awning; but however this which is shaped will suffice for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and remove the purulence, and dry it; and if it is closed, work it, and remove the bowels; then place the hot cauterizer on top, and hold your hand with the cauterizer at the part near the eye, so that your hands do not make a mess; and insert the cauterizer into the fatty part of the eye, and corrupt it.
If you start with the cauterization just up to the bone, it would be good. If not, you should restart the cauterization once again, and if necessary, let it alone for 3 days; then put on moist cotton in butter, and apply the desiccative ointment until it is healed, and if after 40 days it does not heal, put on corrosive medicine until the bone is uncovered; and the rest as you would have come into your chapter.
I have a way of cauterizing a fistula that none of the ancients said. Understand this about the fistula: and cut in its place; then put into the incision 1 subtle thread from a silkworm which is shaped like this: and it is done with the quantity of a .З. of poultice; and hold your hand or camisole firmly, and do not let the patient move until the poultice has set at its oil; and it is necessary to put a moistened cotton cloth over the eye of the patient: the poultice so made is in the place of a fistula, and the blood flows by marvelous sanitation.
And if the fistula is healed by the cauterization that we have said, it is good. If not, it is necessary to administer purgatives to the patient, and bring the fistula back into its proper place in the body of the patient according to what is fitting for its own location.

an abscess of the jaw
When an abscess comes to the root of the jaw or to the gum or palate or to the roots of the teeth and festers and swells up and becomes a fistula; and you treat it as we have said in divisions, but it does not help: then it is necessary that you have a cauterizing iron according to the size of the fistula. Then boil or purge the fistula, and hold your hand until the iron is red hot and at the end of the fistula; and do this once or twice. Afterwards, cure it with what we have said in cures, until it is healed. And if it is cured thus, that's good; and if not, then it is necessary to uncover the place and remove the corrupted bone, according to what you will find in its chapter.
from toothache or tooth sensitivity
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth ache, and you treat it with medicines but it doesn't help, then put the patient's head on your healthy one; afterwards, heat a cauterizing iron whose shape will come out like this: place a cane over the tooth. Then put hot cauterizing iron hastily inside, and hold your hand until the patient feels the heat coming to the root of the tooth. Afterwards, lift your hand and repeat the cauterization according to your will. Afterwards, fill the patient's mouth with salty water, and have them spit it out and rinse several times.
And in this way the tooth that was aching is confirmed to be healed, and the gums that were softened are restored, and the moisture that was corrupting is dried out.
of toothache or dental pain
When toothache comes from cold or there is coldness or heat in the teeth, and medicines do not help, then it is necessary to cauterize according to two ways: cauterizing with butter and cauterizing with fire.
Cauterizing with butter is done like this: you take a piece of cow's butter and shape it into a spoon made of iron or tin. Then take some cotton and wrap it around the point of a tent, and moisten it in melted butter, and apply it hastily to the aching tooth, and hold it until it cools down. And do this often enough that the virtues of the butter come to the root of the tooth. And if you see that the wool and cotton are wet with cold butter, then apply them to the aching tooth, and place a hot iron on top until the fire reaches the gum of the tooth.
Cauterizing with fire is done like this: you take a wooden or metal stick, thick enough that the flame won't reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the cauterizer, and when it reaches the desired shape, place it on the tooth and hold your hand until the cauterizer cools down. Do this often, because the pain will be gone that same day or the next.

when the artery is cut Many times there are great outpourings of blood from the artery that is cut at the wound that appears on the surface, or at the incision of the aposteme, or at the cautery of the limb, and similar things; and it is a very grievous thing to staunch.

Of the lower part of the eye (i.e. the conjunctiva or the sclera).
This illness called "alcunnati" is nothing other than the conjunctiva or the membrane that gathers in the eye, resembling a thread descending, and it's not a thread; the manner of its appearance is as follows: you have the patient sit on a well-made and straight chair, then take his head between your hands from both sides, and lift up his eyelid until you see the conjunctiva or membrane come up to your eyes; then pierce it with a fine needle, and don't let it descend again and be dislodged by the light, and have the patient see things as he normally would; and if it doesn't descend below, know that it's a thread (i.e. not actually conjunctiva), and if the descent of the membrane is not as we said, then have the patient sit in your hands, then take the fine needle and pierce through the corneal membrane one spot at a time, by continuing from the conjunctiva to the cornea and the cornea itself, until the membrane issues; and when it has issued, distill into the eye hot water mixed with honey, or water boiled with fenugreek and honey, then cure him with another treatment until he is healed.

from the skin above the navel

Abscesses of the lips
At home they often get abscesses in the lips that are hard and resemble grains of mustard, smaller and larger ones; then you should turn your lips inside out, and cut each one with a needle and cauterize it with vinegar so that the wound is closed; and then let the lip heal on its own, and treat the area with dry things until it's healed.
from the gum tissue

It seems that you're sharing a description of an ancient medical instrument. Here's my attempt to translate and provide context:
The chapters about abscesses
Many kinds of abscesses are found, according to the remembrance that comes after this in the book, and they differ in perforation and size; and there are two ways in which one is itself similar, and because it retains moisture. And the second way is because of its location or where it is in the body. For the abscesses of the head are different from those of the foundation, and the abscesses of the fatty part are different from those that come to joints, and each has its own judgment in its work.
And if there are abscesses that should not be pierced until the swelling is dead according to its completion; and those of a kind that it is necessary to pierce when they are hard, and do not die according to their completion, such as abscesses near joints. For when an abscess comes near a joint, and you leave it so long that it becomes rotten around it, perhaps the ligaments and nerves of the joint will become corrupted, by which it is the cause of contraction in that member. Or if the abscess is near the principal limb, and you delay perforation until it is dead, it will harm the principal limb; or if it is near the foundation, then pierce it even as soon as it is dead; and it is not necessary to pierce a hard abscess without its digestion being completed, lest that which is near the foundation become rotten deep inside and become elongated, and cause fistulae; and it cannot heal in a long time.
And you must know the sign of perforation of the abscess that is dead according to its completion, and this is when the pain is alleviated, and the departure of fever, and the diminishment of pain and redness, and of fluctuation and of hardening of the head of the abscess, and other signs; and you must do the perforation in a place more dependent on the location of the abscess, and where it seems to be softest; and make the perforation long enough to reach into the body, and if the abscess is in the hands or feet, or near joints and nerves and arteries, and in all places where there is no skin covering.
In those places where the skin is thin, follow the natural contours of the limb. And when an abscess is in a fatty area, it's better to leave the perforation until it's completely digested, as we've said before. For if you pierce them too early, the body will fill with venom and become filled with much foul matter; and perhaps the lips and deeper parts of it will harden.

The boils
They are foul-smelling, large, swollen and full of melancholy pus, which come on one or more members of the body.
and they are more likely to occur in the legs of those who walk and work and carry loads, than in others.
Therefore you should administer to them an occasional purging with vinegar, and then salve with the oil of basil.
The treatment of iron wounds is in two ways: one is to bind them and draw out the black blood. and the other way is that you lift up the boil, and drain it completely.
The cutting of a boil is done in this way: first, take the leg of a hot iron very firmly, until the thick blood is thinned. then apply the heated iron to the patient's leg from above the knee all the way down to the part below the kneecap. then cut the boil in one place, or in two places, or in three, with a wide cut.
then lift it up and drain out the black blood into your hand, from one end to the other, until as much of the bad stuff comes out as the patient can stand.
and then bind it and command that he eat meat in anointing black vinegar;
and repeat the evacuation and salving when the boils are full-grown and painful to the patient.

Of the worms that are born between skin and flesh
This disease is called in our land "bovine sickness", because it has often occurred among oxen.
and it is a small worm that is born between skin and flesh, and goes up and down through the whole body. and one feels it very well as it moves from member to member, until it reaches the skin and bursts out.
and it is inflamed from putridity of certain humors, such as come from worms and snakes and lice and ascarids or from the belly.
and one doubts about its evilness, because when it goes through the body, and it rises to the head, and it comes to the eye. perhaps it enters and destroys the eye; and this has happened many times.
When you see it being cured and drained, one can hardly tell that it was there:
so it is good that you bind the area above and below with a strong thread tightly.
then cut on top and drain outwards, and if the worm is deep in the flesh and you cannot find it, then cauterize with fire above, until it blackens.
and the greatest harm that it does is that it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
and if you see it coming near the head close to the eye, then bind a thread under it in a circle, then cut on top and drain outwards.
and it is good to use the sick person with purifying medicines for the putrid humors, to cleanse the body.
and abstain from meat that causes putrefaction.
Of the pain that comes from windiness or flatulence. This disease is called in our land "vakirir", and it is a pain that comes to certain members, and then moves from member to member; and I have seen what I am telling you.
I was called to a sick woman, and she showed me her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm.
and after a little while, the vein rose up like a worm to the shoulder. sooner than anything, just as quicksilver when it runs from one place to another;
the pain departs from that place and settles in the shoulder.
and then after a little while it ran through the whole body, so that it came to the other arm.
and then she told me that it was turning around the body, just as I saw it.
I was greatly amazed at how it moved from member to member, for I had never seen this disease in such a way before. However, I had often heard that the pain could move from one place to another, but I had never seen it like this, as I did in the woman.
and I thought that it was because the woman's body was dry and her veins were uncovered, and that is why the windiness appeared to be constrained.
and it does not appear in this way among those who are luscious and have moist bodies and closed veins.

Of the splinters or thorns that have entered into the body.
Splinters are diverse, according to their appearances and according to the places in the body where they lodge.
The diversity of them is that there are those with large points and small ones, and thorny, and splintered. and those with two edges, and those with tongues, and those that have fragments. and the one that lodges in any member lodges in either of two ways: or it lodges in a main member, and becomes lodged there.
such as the brain, heart, liver, spleen, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and similar members. The book of wounds begins here. (Note: this likely marks the end of the section on ventouses and the beginning of a new chapter in the medical text)
This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine, and is a treatment for wounds and swellings.
Know, dear son, that foolish men scorn this chapter, having never seen any of the old books nor read a letter about it.
and because of this reason is the knowledge estranged in our land, for I have never found any good one.
and I know nothing else, except by the knowledge of the old books, in which I have studied so much that I have treated the science, and by experience, and by the custom of my life.
and I have written in this chapter what is contained, and shortened it at the end, and have shown clearly the forms of the instruments that are brought, as I have done in the two chapters before this one.
It is therefore necessary that we say the prologue of this book, which contains the sum of words and chapters where the wise man sets his understanding, and remains on the truth, so that those who are proven know and learn this noble art, and teach it to others.
I say that when any breakage or dislocation or twisting or crushing comes to someone, it is necessary that you hasten to cure the ailing person on one hand and, if nothing else is the case, such as weakness of body, or infancy, or old age;
or if the body's temper is very cold or too hot; and in case of cold fever, it means cold juices, such as bird and goat meat, and do not drink wine, nor eat greasy foods, and do not fill yourself too much with meats that fill the veins with blood, until you have no doubt about a warm abscess or bursting of matter into the member; then let the patient return to his previous treatment right away.
And when the broken bone starts to heal, it is necessary that one nourishes the patient with meats that make thick nourishment, or have viscosity, such as boiled wheat, rice, cheese, and veal, and eggs, and fresh fish, and rich wine, and similar things. For with proper nourishment the bone heals more quickly and sooner the breakage.
And know that the broken bones of the old and decrepit cannot be well reunited or mended according to their natural order because of the weariness of the members, nor those also who are too soft, such as the bones of small children.
but there is no cure, scrape off on the broken bone, that is to say, large scabs which have formed and joined the member together, as long as it strengthens itself like before; and do not remove them completely, and therefore it is necessary to nourish with thick meats, as we have said.
And know that breakages are diverse in many ways.
For the breakage of the head is different from the breakage of the thigh or leg, and I will tell you in this chapter all the ways of breaking. For there are breaks that bend (or fracture) at a curve, and others in a crackling manner, such as the breakage of a quill pen for writing.
For there are breaks that bend (or fracture) at a curve, and others in a crackling manner, such as the breakage of a quill pen for writing. And also a break with an open wound, and a break with a small crack or fissure throughout its entire length.
And each type has its own remedy and proper instrument in restoration, according to what the memory dictates in its place.
and it is known that a break of the bone by this, that the place is twisted and opens well from outside, and sounds when pressed with the hand.
and if there is no twist or sound at the touch, and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no break.
but it can well have a twist, crack, or small fracture. So you should not move the hand at all, but put on medications that come next, and then bind it now gently.
And know that the break that bends in a curve, and where the two parts are disjointed, it is necessary to bind them now so that they meet equally again, just as if an abscess were to come.
and if the abscess comes, then leave it alone for a day, until the abscess is calmed down. Then bind it equally to the bandages that you put lightly and gently, and know that the restoration of this one is easier than the restorations of those where there are splinters; then bind it, as it is necessary next.
And if there are broken pieces in the bone, and they extend into two parts, such as hands or feet, or if it is in your hand, then it is a small member, or if it is to two strings, or to one string and your hand;

sur les plaies qui s'ouvrent.
Nous avons souvent affaire après la guérison des blessures, en particulier lorsque celle-ci se trouve près d'une articulation, et c'est une chose triste à voir la figure du membre; et peut-être que le membre ne pourra plus faire ses opérations naturelles.
Prends garde donc à te faire une sorte de pansement : si tu administres des médicines stiptiques, comme l'aloès, l'encens, la myrrhe, le sarcocole, l'acacie et semblables autres, en sorte que tu prennes aucune de ces choses ou toutes ensemble, et les confites avec du vin stiptique ou avec l'abond d'œuf ou avec l'ail ; et met-les sur la blessure en estoupes, et enstrains-les bien, et ne les desliers pas en mains jusqu'à ce qu'ils soient séchés ; puis les déloies, et recommences une autre, tant que les nodations soient ôtées. Ou tu les enstrains sur une pièce de plonc bien faite : car la propriété du plonc est qu'il ôte toutes les enfleures qui sont sur les membres ; et si la nodation est déjà convertie en dureté comme la pierre, et il est besoin de l'ôter, si tu la coupes dessus, et fais la superfluité apparaître ; ou tu la réfais à un chirurgien, tant qu'elle soit ôtée, et la cure tant qu'elle soit saine.
Lorsque la fracture de l'os est réparée et que le membre reste faible et délicat, il peut être dû à plusieurs causes. D'une part, c'est parce que la ligature était trop serrée ou pas suffisamment serrée comme il convenait ; ou bien il y avait trop de filaments qui empêchaient le rétablissement du membre ; ou en raison de multiples saignements ; ou en raison d'un mouvement trop grand et non dans son temps ; ou parce que la personne avait peu de sang dans son corps, ce qui fait qu'elle est faible.
Et la cure consiste à faire manger le malade pour renforcer son corps et rendre-le tendre, jusqu'à ce que le sang se multiplie en lui. On lui administre un bain et on lui donne joie et liesse. Puis on met des cataplasmes sur le membre, de telle sorte que la pommade attire un grand rétablissement et l'ardeur souvent d'une teintede, jusqu'à ce que le rétablissement arrive au membre ; et ainsi il revient à sa figure naturelle.
